    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer6326817756738856268">At a café table in Lahore, a bearded Pakistani man converses with an uneasy American stranger. As dusk deepens to night, he begins the tale that has brought them to this fateful encounter…<br/><br/>Changez is living an immigrant's dream of America. At the top of his class at Princeton, he is snapped up by an elite valuation firm. He thrives on the energy of New York, and his budding</span>
  <span id="freeText6326817756738856268" style="display:none">At a café table in Lahore, a bearded Pakistani man converses with an uneasy American stranger. As dusk deepens to night, he begins the tale that has brought them to this fateful encounter…<br /><br />Changez is living an immigrant's dream of America. At the top of his class at Princeton, he is snapped up by an elite valuation firm. He thrives on the energy of New York, and his budding romance with elegant, beautiful Erica promises entry into Manhattan society at the same exalted level once occupied by his own family back in Lahore.<br /><br />But in the wake of September 11, Changez finds his position in his adopted city suddenly overturned, and his relationship with Erica shifting. And Changez's own identity is in seismic shift as well, unearthing allegiances more fundamental than money, power, and maybe even love.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6326817756738856268" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a href="/questions/1249669-i-hated-the-ending-either-he-is-working"><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I hated the ending. Either he is working for the dark side and luring the guy into an ambush or he is having a genuine and generous cross cultural chat. There's hints that either could be the correct ending. If you're telling a story - tell the story. Anyone agree? <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></a>

<div class='spoiler'>

<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  
    au contraire - I thought the ending was really well done. Stories don't always have tidy stop points. This strikes me as a perceptive rendition of a p<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">…more</a>
    au contraire - I thought the ending was really well done. Stories don't always have tidy stop points. This strikes me as a perceptive rendition of a prevalent American paranoid attitude towards "exotics." The reader absolutely can defend either - or any other - speculation as to what happens next. I spent a little while trying to figure it out but then realized I didn't really need to.<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">(less)</a>
  
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>

<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a href="/questions/1037689-who-do-you-think-the-waiter-was-or-was"><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Who do you think the waiter was, or was representing, at the end? My thought was that the listening man was CIA, come to "take care of" Changez, but, if so, I wasn't sure who the waiter might be. Hope this isn't a dumb question--I'm not good at parsing out the underlying meaning of stuff! <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></a>

<span class='communityAnswererName'>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<div class='spoiler'>
This answer contains spoilers…
<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  
    At first I thought that the listening man was CIA, but then I thought that (maybe) Changez was too low level to be of interest. However, he notes at t<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">…more</a>
    At first I thought that the listening man was CIA, but then I thought that (maybe) Changez was too low level to be of interest. However, he notes at the end that he did appear to interest the U.S. government. I ended up concluding that the listening man was a random American and Changez is working with the waiter to rob/beat up rich Americans and steal from them (or worse?). The listening man even notes that the two exchanged a signal. That's my interpretation.<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">(less)</a>
  
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>
</span>

<div id="review_332048462" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/332048462" />
    <a title="Prashant" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6303222-prashant"><img alt="Prashant" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/332048462?book_show_action=true">May 18, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Prashant" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Prashant" href="/user/show/6303222-prashant">Prashant</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780151013043" href="/book/show/88815.The_Reluctant_Fundamentalist">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer332048462" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1478630914946205013"><b>
  <u> At a Bookstore in India </u>
</b><br/><br/>Sir, I see that you are checking out this book by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/16902.Mohsin_Hamid' title='Mohsin Hamid' rel='nofollow noopener'>Mohsin Hamid</a> . I read it a few days back. How did I find it you ask? Well, it was pretty interesting. I found the narration style of the author quite unique. I think that alone was reason enough to make it worth. <br/><br/>Oh, you are getting distracted. I see you are eyeing those shining new book covers of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2767052.The_Hunger_Games__The_Hunger_Games___1_' title='The Hunger Games (The Hunger Games, #1) by Suzanne Collins' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Hunger Games</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2429135.The_Girl_With_the_Dragon_Tattoo__Millennium___1_' title='The Girl With the Dragon Tattoo (Millennium, #1) by Stieg Larsson' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Girl with the Dragon Tattoo</a>. They came out after the movies were released. No, I have not seen th</span>
  <span id="freeText1478630914946205013" style="display:none"><b>
  <u> At a Bookstore in India </u>
</b><br /><br />Sir, I see that you are checking out this book by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/16902.Mohsin_Hamid" title="Mohsin Hamid" rel="nofollow noopener">Mohsin Hamid</a> . I read it a few days back. How did I find it you ask? Well, it was pretty interesting. I found the narration style of the author quite unique. I think that alone was reason enough to make it worth. <br /><br />Oh, you are getting distracted. I see you are eyeing those shining new book covers of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2767052.The_Hunger_Games__The_Hunger_Games___1_" title="The Hunger Games (The Hunger Games, #1) by Suzanne Collins" rel="nofollow noopener">The Hunger Games</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2429135.The_Girl_With_the_Dragon_Tattoo__Millennium___1_" title="The Girl With the Dragon Tattoo (Millennium, #1) by Stieg Larsson" rel="nofollow noopener">The Girl with the Dragon Tattoo</a>. They came out after the movies were released. No, I have not seen the movies but it’s good to hear that you found them intriguing. Are you looking forward to reading the books? That’s very good. I found the books impressive but just my experience with movies inspired from books made me avoid the movies. <br /><br />It’s okay that you are a casual reader. Nowadays most of us are. I see in your grip on the book and the way your eyes exuded enthusiasm when you saw the new cover, that you like to buy books. <br /><br />What prompted you to find this book then? You heard it from a friend and he recommended it to you. Very wise of him/her, I must say. You see, this book is about a young Pakistani who went to the US for studies and later lived there with a very lucrative job. He detailed the events of his life like finding his first job, his first crush which later became love and the twists that happened post 9/11. <br /><br />Your eyes are drifting. I think you are becoming bored. You feel this story line has been covered umpteen times in many books. I must give you something which makes this one different? <br /><br />I say, it’s the simplicity of this story. The emotions displayed by the younger generation of the third world countries when they work for the very country which is making the life of their own people back home miserable. This is a new breed that has worked their brains hard, can speak English effortlessly and are starting with salaries which are much more than their father's. <br /><br />Now you are able to connect with it. You are turning some pages to look if you find it interesting.Reading a full page from the middle of the book is also a good idea. What did you find so astonishing here? Yes, he had a conversation with some man who changed his heart. But let me tell you that he was not a religious fanatic. <br /><br />No it won’t turn into the usual brainwashing of the literate story. Here, you will see some soft emotions. I have to admit the ending is not so interesting but I hope that won’t make the whole book useless for you. <br /><br />In meeting you the pleasure was all mine. I like to talk to people about books. Many times you will find me standing alone at this or the other corner of the book store. Would you like to recommend some book to me? The next time do tell me about your favorite books. <br /><br />You are buying this book. I hope you will not regret it. Let me know how you like it. :)<br /></span>

<div id="review_24038317" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/24038317" />
    <a title="Bookchica" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/643419-bookchica"><img alt="Bookchica" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/24038317?book_show_action=true">Jun 08, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bookchica" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bookchica" href="/user/show/643419-bookchica">Bookchica</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780151013043" href="/book/show/88815.The_Reluctant_Fundamentalist">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">all</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer24038317" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8355012878563981699">On a flight back to US from India, about half an hour was left to land in San Francisco, everyone was asleep, when we heard the captain speaking over the intercom. All I heard was something about how we were about to land in Japan. In my sleepy state I assumed that something was wrong with the plane and was about to panic when my husband told me the rest of the captain's message. Apparently we were denied entry into United States because a passenger was on their no-fly list.<br/><br/>On landing in Japan, </span>
  <span id="freeText8355012878563981699" style="display:none">On a flight back to US from India, about half an hour was left to land in San Francisco, everyone was asleep, when we heard the captain speaking over the intercom. All I heard was something about how we were about to land in Japan. In my sleepy state I assumed that something was wrong with the plane and was about to panic when my husband told me the rest of the captain's message. Apparently we were denied entry into United States because a passenger was on their no-fly list.<br /><br />On landing in Japan, as we all emptied the plane, I saw a family of about 6 - a young boy, bearded, about 20, and women of different ages wearing burkha's - sitting quietly in the center seats not meeting anyones eyes. I remarked to my husband about how horrid they must be feeling, that just because they are Muslims they must have shown up on the security radar for US. Once the aircraft had been emptied out, the family was brought out with about 10 men surrounding them and taken away. We boarded the plane again and went on our way. Once there, we told our friends about our "adventure" and had discussions about racial profiling, heard stories from others about how they had been subjected to profiling. Pro's and con's of racial profiling, US government, security, prejudice, patriotism, terrorism.. I'm sure you can all imagine what was discussed and debated. I remember sympathizing with the family on the plane.<br /><br />About a week later, I read that the young boy had later been sent to US and arrested on arrival. Allegedly he had gone to Pakistan and had taken part in a terrorist camp. I did not follow the case since then.<br /><br />Those of you who are still reading this post, thanks :). Throughout the book, as I heard Changez (the young Pakistani protagonist) talk about his life in America, I followed him on all the various issues he tackles in the book. Be it his social identity, his professional acceptance, America's fair treatment to him and his achievements. But as I finished the book, my thoughts forked out to this incident.<br />I don't know what happened to the boy in the plane. How accurate were the accusations? Did he or why did he join a camp and many questions that went through my mind. Many that would remain unanswered. I did wish Mohsin Hamid had ended the book on a definite note, but then that would have made it more fictional than real in account.<br /><br />This extremely fluid, unapologetic, frank, point of view had me hooked from page 1. Changez a young muslim, confident, achiever, confused, looking for acceptance, searching for identity, guilty of abandoning family, trying to define his patriotism, enjoying the fruits of his labor - all his layers come through with such clarity. I really enjoyed the narrative style. It flowed naturally. It felt like you were right there listening in on an actual conversation.<br /><br />Mohsin Hamid has not held back Changez's thoughts to be politically correct, or tried to portray Changez as a victim. The Reluctant Fundamentalist is an honest, at times appealing and at times disconcerting, account of a man's internal thoughts, who knows that he may be a few feet away from death and has nothing to lose by telling all.<br /><br />For more reviews, go to <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://ireadokay.blogspot.com">http://ireadokay.blogspot.com</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="8355012878563981699" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_126974371" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/126974371" />
    <a title="Paul Bryant" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/416390-paul-bryant"><img alt="Paul Bryant" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1224113172p2/416390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/126974371?book_show_action=true">Oct 19, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul Bryant" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul Bryant" href="/user/show/416390-paul-bryant">Paul Bryant</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141029542" href="/book/show/2163164.The_Reluctant_Fundamentalist">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/416390-paul-bryant?shelf=novels">novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer126974371" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10283937432528239427">A real bowl of literary prawn crackers - you eat and eat and they taste of nothing, they're entirely synthetic, like a form of extruded plastic, but you can't stop and then you realise the whole bowl is gone and what was that all about? This is not a good book and yet it was compelling, I can't deny it, a smooth, snaky insinuating monologue which in retrospect and often in real-time spect is a ridiculous tissue of allegory, you've seen all this in other reviews but it's all horribly true - our r</span>
  <span id="freeText10283937432528239427" style="display:none">A real bowl of literary prawn crackers - you eat and eat and they taste of nothing, they're entirely synthetic, like a form of extruded plastic, but you can't stop and then you realise the whole bowl is gone and what was that all about? This is not a good book and yet it was compelling, I can't deny it, a smooth, snaky insinuating monologue which in retrospect and often in real-time spect is a ridiculous tissue of allegory, you've seen all this in other reviews but it's all horribly true - our reluctant hero's name is Changez - that's right<br /><br />Ch-ch-ch-changez to you!<br /><br />and his svelte not-quite-attainable lurve is (Am) Erica<br /><br />I hope Mohsin Hamid wakes up in hot sweats in the middle of the night thinking Oh God, how could I have done that!<br /><br />The fundamentalism of the title is from the business slogan of the arbitrage company he works for in New York, "focus on the fundamentals" - that's the fundamentalism he's reluctant about. Okay, nice joke.<br /><br />That said, a lot of the reviews of this book would have you believe it's an apology for al Qaeda - no, it's not, Changez is an extraordinarily secular Muslim - I think the M word is used once only in the whole book, and nowhere does he speak of Islam. The opposition to America which is eventually accepted and embodied by our troubled young man is entirely political - he does give a faint but pertinent impression of America as the lover who kills you or as the murderer who loves you. But oh dear, this kind of thing is not good :<br /><br /> "I had always resented the manner in which America conducted itself in the world; your country's constant interference in the affairs of others was insufferable. Vietnam, Korea, the straits of Taiwan ..."<br /><br />I thought there was so much more to be said about how the East wants what the West has got without wanting to be colonised and disembowelled by the West, and how America is the very embodiment of guilty pleasure, and how this love hate thing is like to drive entire countryfuls of young men raving mad, given the repressive anti-sex poverty-stricken societies they come from, and how this explains a whole lot, but Hamsid's touch was so light you could almost have mistaken it for shallowness. <br /><br />Two and a half.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10283937432528239427" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bill Kerwin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bill Kerwin" href="/user/show/83582-bill-kerwin">Bill Kerwin</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer1107711596437514404"><br/>In one sustained monologue, a young Pakistani named Changez relates his life story to an unidentified American man in a cafe in the city of Lahore. Changez, a Princeton graduate who once worked as an analyst for a Manhattan financial firm, tells us how his optimistic view of America began to darken in the aftermath of 9/11. <br/><br/>I liked this book for its elegant style and outsider's viewpoint, but my favorite part of it is the mysterious relationship between the narrator and his American listener. Te</span>
  <span id="freeText1107711596437514404" style="display:none"><br />In one sustained monologue, a young Pakistani named Changez relates his life story to an unidentified American man in a cafe in the city of Lahore. Changez, a Princeton graduate who once worked as an analyst for a Manhattan financial firm, tells us how his optimistic view of America began to darken in the aftermath of 9/11. <br /><br />I liked this book for its elegant style and outsider's viewpoint, but my favorite part of it is the mysterious relationship between the narrator and his American listener. Tension and threat bubble beneath the novel's polite surface, and the possible explanations for that tension keep the reader guessing and give the novel sublety, power and depth.</span>

<div id="review_14252380" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/14252380" />
    <a title="Sandhya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/215382-sandhya"><img alt="Sandhya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1244981990p2/215382.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/14252380?book_show_action=true">Feb 01, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sandhya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sandhya" href="/user/show/215382-sandhya">Sandhya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780151013043" href="/book/show/88815.The_Reluctant_Fundamentalist">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer14252380" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7746227596748217904">I've been trying to read some good Pakistani writing in English for a while now. And I'm glad I made an introduction with Mohsin Hamid's The Reluctant Fundamentalist, who earlier wrote Moth Smoke, a novel, which Rahul Bose is now adapting into a film.<br/><br/>Lately, there has been a flowering of young Pakistani writers like Hamid and Kamila Shamsie (Cartography, Salt And Saffron), and in many ways, this is the first literary stirring that the country is witnessing.<br/><br/>The Reluctant Fundamentalist looks at t</span>
  <span id="freeText7746227596748217904" style="display:none">I've been trying to read some good Pakistani writing in English for a while now. And I'm glad I made an introduction with Mohsin Hamid's The Reluctant Fundamentalist, who earlier wrote Moth Smoke, a novel, which Rahul Bose is now adapting into a film.<br /><br />Lately, there has been a flowering of young Pakistani writers like Hamid and Kamila Shamsie (Cartography, Salt And Saffron), and in many ways, this is the first literary stirring that the country is witnessing.<br /><br />The Reluctant Fundamentalist looks at the increasingly volatile and precariously balanced relationship between the West (United States) and East (South Asian Muslim countries), and how without a certain sense empathy, this equation will steadily spiral downwards.<br /><br />Interestingly, Hamid’s point here is that a feeling of fundamentalism can arise in the unlikeliest of people, when they feel pushed to a corner.<br /><br />The novel’s protagonist, Changez is a Princeton graduate, has led a charmed life back in Pakistan and is all set for a enviable career in New York.<br />He bags a job with one of the premium companies of the city, Underwood Samson and in a short while, is recogonised as one of the firm’s brightest young talents.<br /><br />If he thought life couldn't get better, he’s proved wrong. Soon enough, he falls in love with Erica, a rich, pretty and artistically inclined American girl. But this relationship is fraught with troubles. Though there is a great deal of affection and even curiosity between Changez and Erica about their respective backgrounds, theirs remains a largely unfulfilling bond. Erica cannot get over Chris, her boyfriend who died some years back and thereby, can never fully 'open up' (sexually too) with Changez. In a moment of frustration and even resentment, the latter asks her to imagine him as Chris and make love.<br /><br />This is when you realize that Hamid’s constructed an allegory here. Erica stands for America (Erica), and symbolises the deep infactuation Changez feels for her on certain levels. His own company is called Underwood Sampsons, standing for US, a highly competitive firm with a narrow focus on its own progress.<br /><br />Erica's inability to accept Changez, unless he 'becomes' Chris, quite clearly, hints at the country's unwillingness to accept the former’s identity for what it primarily is.<br /><br />Till this point, Changez largely shares a love-hate equation with the US. He loves being a New Yorker, both his high-flying job and girlfriend fill his heart with a sense of pride. However, at the same time, Hamid's protagonist is no pushover. Clearly, Changez has a mind of his own and feels a deep sense of attachment to his motherland (Pakistan). The fact that bright minds like him have to desert their own country, to fill the coffers of an already overdeveloped, supercilious country, leaves him frustrated.<br /><br />This realisation further dawns upon him when 9/11 occurs and Changez feels a strange sense of thrill at 'someone bringing America to its knees'. From there on, life is never the same and his disenchantment with America is complete.<br /><br />Erica is afflicted with a mental illness and slowly fades away (literally) from his life. This is a period when Changez also develops a certain rebellious streak, refusing to either cut off his beard or focus on his job. News of America's attacks on Afghanistan, Pakistan's closest neighbour fills his heart with resentment and from there on, it's only a matter of time before he loses his job.<br />Once back in Pakistan, Changez becomes a professor at a University, 'who makes it his mission on the campus to advocate Pakistan's disengagement with America'<br /><br />Though the book does not, in any way, glorify fundamentalism, it subtly points at how sparks of fundamentalism can be ignited in the most placid looking people and circumstances. Hamid succeeds in making his central character-Changez engaging from the word go and it helps that this book is a rather compact, slim one, without too much rambling. <br />But, while Hamid's attempt at constructing an allegorical narrative is interesting, it is hardly intrusive enough to lend the story any kind of depth. If anything, it slackens its dramatic pace, making it both tedious and essayist.<br />On the other hand, Changez's professional life has been treated with great flair and understanding.<br />There are great stories to be written on the increasing east-west gulf and the growing feelings of mistrust between both continents. The Reluctant Fundamentalist only skims the surface, but nevertheless Hamid does enough to prove that he's a writer to watch out for.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7746227596748217904" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3018074" />
    <a title="Sanjay" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/65107-sanjay"><img alt="Sanjay" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3018074?book_show_action=true">Jul 13, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sanjay" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sanjay" href="/user/show/65107-sanjay">Sanjay</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780151013043" href="/book/show/88815.The_Reluctant_Fundamentalist">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3018074" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5222590746158351475">Nine Reasons To Read This One:<br/><br/>Because it’s short, yet evocative: a relief at a time when authors needlessly pile on the pages.<br/><br/>Because it’s hard enough to sustain a distinctive voice for a dramatic monologue in a poem (ask Robert Browning), leave alone an entire novel.<br/><br/>Because the voice is just right – formal without being sombre; precise without being stiff.<br/><br/>Because, unlike in John Updike’s Terrorist, you can empathise with and understand Changez, the fundamentalist.<br/><br/>Because of the delicious ironie</span>
  <span id="freeText5222590746158351475" style="display:none">Nine Reasons To Read This One:<br /><br />Because it’s short, yet evocative: a relief at a time when authors needlessly pile on the pages.<br /><br />Because it’s hard enough to sustain a distinctive voice for a dramatic monologue in a poem (ask Robert Browning), leave alone an entire novel.<br /><br />Because the voice is just right – formal without being sombre; precise without being stiff.<br /><br />Because, unlike in John Updike’s Terrorist, you can empathise with and understand Changez, the fundamentalist.<br /><br />Because of the delicious ironies, among them the fact that Changez works in a US firm that evaluates companies ripe for takeover; virtually the first piece of advice he receives is to stick to the fundamentals.<br /><br />Because Changez’s disillusionment comes about in a nuanced, progressive manner and as such is completely believable.<br /><br />Because there’s ample evidence of the author’s craft, especially in Changez’s many responses and descriptions while narrating his tale in a Lahore bazaar.<br /><br />Because yet another example of such craft is that Changez’s ill-fated relationship with the USA is matched by his ill-fated relationship with Erica – without being heavy-handed about it.<br /><br />Because in less than 200 pages, Hamid creates both a compelling protagonist and a compelling argument.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5222590746158351475" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_32980339" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/32980339" />
    <a title="Will Byrnes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1526851-will-byrnes"><img alt="Will Byrnes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1291462190p2/1526851.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/32980339?book_show_action=true">Sep 15, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Will Byrnes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Will Byrnes" href="/user/show/1526851-will-byrnes">Will Byrnes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780151013043" href="/book/show/88815.The_Reluctant_Fundamentalist">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1526851-will-byrnes?shelf=terrorism">terrorism</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer32980339&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer32980339" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17152449359823795667">This is a lovely, short, very easy-to-read post 9/11 book. <br/><br/>The structure of this is tale is Changez telling his personal story to a burly American visitor (probably a spook of some sort) to his country, in his function as a guide to Pakistan. The tone was very reminiscent of Rudyard Kipling, at least as far as I recall from my reading of Kipling many years back. Think The Man Who Would Be King. This makes sense given the subject matter of the book, colonialism versus the third world. <br/><br/>Changez, bo</span>
  <span id="freeText17152449359823795667" style="display:none">This is a lovely, short, very easy-to-read post 9/11 book. <br /><br />The structure of this is tale is Changez telling his personal story to a burly American visitor (probably a spook of some sort) to his country, in his function as a guide to Pakistan. The tone was very reminiscent of Rudyard Kipling, at least as far as I recall from my reading of Kipling many years back. Think The Man Who Would Be King. This makes sense given the subject matter of the book, colonialism versus the third world. <br /><br />Changez, born to fading gentry in Pakistan, has attended Princeton on scholarship, gotten a lucrative job with a top tier financial company, and is in love with beautiful, blond upper-class Yank. Life is good. But when 9/11 happens he discovers that he feels some satisfaction in the great giant being taken down a notch. In the newly paranoid USA, his background marks him as a threat to many and life changes. <br /><br />Essentially what we have here is a foreigner (Changez) falling in love with America (get it? amERICA), but his amERICA is too damaged by the premature loss of her boyfriend to cancer at age 22 (Read Vietnam or whatever other fall one might choose) to cope. The result of this is that amERICA suffers from extreme nostalgia and becomes incapable of truly embracing Changez (subtle). <br /><br />Erica’s father irks him with presumptions about corruption in Pakistan. He sees a “typically American undercurrent of condescension” (p 55) American indifference to third world concerns is noted repeatedly here. It is no secret that the USA is notoriously unempathetic to the concerns of others since the Marshall Plan. <br /><br />Fundamentals here are the tools taught him in his finance career (efficiency). Fundamentals are implied for other things, knowing who you are, what your place is in the world. There are, surprisingly, no overt connections made to religious fundamentalism. Presumably one of the author’s points is that the values held high in the west (efficiency uber alles) are just as unfeeling and extreme as those of the religious nuts. <br /><br />I did not take this as a personal tale. It is a metaphoric one. I mean the main character has but a single name, Changez. For that alone, how could the book be anything other than metaphorical? So I was not troubled by the contradictions in the character. For example, Changez feels an affinity with the jeepney driver in the Philippines, yet the choices he makes are all to strive within the western world. He manages to get a scholarship to attend Princeton, but feels it necessary to hide his relative poverty. What? Are there no other scholarship kids at Princeton? He is elitist in his orientation, wanting to hang with the rich kids, wanting to work for the heavy hitter financial company, even after it becomes clear to him that the work will cost people their livelihoods, wanting to be with the crazy girl when it is clear that she is over the edge. It is not America that rejects the foreigner here, but the foreigner who rejects America. So it is not a personal tale. It is a metaphoric one. It would have been better had the walking symbols here been made more reasonable, had their desires and impulses been a little more grounded in flesh and blood reality. <br /><br />You’re not a better man than I am, Gunga Din.<br /><br /><br />=============================<b>EXTRA STUFF</b><br /><br />Hamid's <a href="http://www.mohsinhamid.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">personal</a>, <a href="https://www.facebook.com/MohsinHamidOfficialPage" rel="nofollow noopener">FB</a>, <a href="https://twitter.com/mohsin_hamid" rel="nofollow noopener">twitter</a> pages<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17152449359823795667" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_59534456" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/59534456" />
    <a title="DoctorM" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/175916-doctorm"><img alt="DoctorM" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1289615196p2/175916.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/59534456?book_show_action=true">Jun 13, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="DoctorM" class="user" itemprop="url" name="DoctorM" href="/user/show/175916-doctorm">DoctorM</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780151013043" href="/book/show/88815.The_Reluctant_Fundamentalist">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/175916-doctorm?shelf=summer-year-nine-list">summer-year-nine-list</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/175916-doctorm?shelf=dar-al-islam">dar-al-islam</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/175916-doctorm?shelf=out-in-the-colonies">out-in-the-colonies</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer59534456" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2683602576722484734">An eerie, quietly powerful story. The structure is simple enough--- a monologue. A cafe in Lahore, and a young Pakistani is explaining to a silent American how he came to be an enemy of America. There's menace there--- something is about to happen, and soon. You're not told why the American is there, or what he does, or quite why young Changez is telling him these things. But there it is. This voice--- educated, articulate, tinged with hostility and faux-bonhomie and self-pity ---speaking into t</span>
  <span id="freeText2683602576722484734" style="display:none">An eerie, quietly powerful story. The structure is simple enough--- a monologue. A cafe in Lahore, and a young Pakistani is explaining to a silent American how he came to be an enemy of America. There's menace there--- something is about to happen, and soon. You're not told why the American is there, or what he does, or quite why young Changez is telling him these things. But there it is. This voice--- educated, articulate, tinged with hostility and faux-bonhomie and self-pity ---speaking into the dusk, ordering more tea, and...waiting. <br /><br />There are reviewers at GoodReads who just didn't <i>get</i> the narrator, who just disliked him out of hand. After all, they said--- full scholarship to Princeton, near-six-figure Wall Street job at 22, beautiful American girlfriend: how dare he dislike America? I just kept reading and thinking about Dostoyevsky's "Devils" or Conrad's "Under Western Eyes". Changez would be...exactly...the sort to end up a terrorist of some kind. From a family with old name and status but no money. Educated someplace where you're <i>almost</i> never aware of being different, where suddenly money is an issue, where status and formalized deference don't soften the edges of not having money. A job with travel to places where you're aware of being American in the eyes of locals, but being a mere foreigner to American customs officials. Being smitten with a beautiful, gentle Upper East Side girl who slips away from you. Changez turns on the TV in a Manila hotel suite and sees the Towers burning on 11 September 2001 and finds himself suddenly, unexpectedly...smiling. However not? You can see Changez being as surprised as any of his American employers and friends at just how much resentment is there. Just the sort of person who could be recruited, who'd find himself seeking out places where he could open up his anger. <br /><br />There's no grand political justification here, no sudden acceptance of Islam or jihad. Changez is secular, and his disdain for Americans isn't religious as much it is based on tribe and class and a sense of falling between identities. Mohsin Hamid gives his narrator a disturbing and quiet sense of slowly growing bitterness and isolation, as well as a slowly growing desperation about finding an identity. <i> I am a Kurtz</i>, he tells his nameless American listener, <i>waiting for my Marlowe.</i> <br /><br />"The Reluctant Fundamentalist" is an unexpected find, and a story with a whole cast of ghosts and ideas happening just behind the main narrative. Very much worth reading, and a book where you'll be uncovering layers in Changez's monologue for a long time. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2683602576722484734" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1310975017" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1310975017" />
    <a title="Ted" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7213075-ted"><img alt="Ted" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1553101210p2/7213075.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1310975017?book_show_action=true">Apr 19, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ted" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ted" href="/user/show/7213075-ted">Ted</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780151013043" href="/book/show/88815.The_Reluctant_Fundamentalist">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7213075-ted?shelf=have">have</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7213075-ted?shelf=reviews-liked">reviews-liked</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7213075-ted?shelf=lit-asian">lit-asian</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1310975017" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6801738685969626702"><b>One of the most contentiously rated novels I've seen here</b><br/><br/><br/>I'd had the book for 2-3 years probably, when, a couple months ago, I determined that I needed to make shelf space. This was one of a few books I decided to get rid of, even though it was unread. But it was so short, and I had looked forward to reading it ...<br/><br/>So I put it beside books I was reading and would soon read, then picked it up a few nights ago when I was tired but didn't feel like going to bed, and started reading. I’m very glad I </span>
  <span id="freeText6801738685969626702" style="display:none"><b>One of the most contentiously rated novels I've seen here</b><br /><br /><br />I'd had the book for 2-3 years probably, when, a couple months ago, I determined that I needed to make shelf space. This was one of a few books I decided to get rid of, even though it was unread. But it was so short, and I had looked forward to reading it ...<br /><br />So I put it beside books I was reading and would soon read, then picked it up a few nights ago when I was tired but didn't feel like going to bed, and started reading. I’m very glad I did.<br /><br />As soon as I'd read a couple pages I was interested. Can't recall reading a story in this narrative style. It's all in the first person, the words are being spoken by the narrator, Changez, to an American man, never named, whose apparently only occasional words are never explicitly heard, simply acknowledged in the narration by something like,<br /><br />Oh, but you mustn't assume that I believed that, sir. <i>or</i> What's that you say, sir? You'd like something to drink? How would some nice tea do for you? Fine, I'd like a cup too, I'll order for us.<br /><br />The entire almost one-sided conversation takes place over the course of several hours, from mid-afternoon perhaps to late at night. In it, the Pakistani narrator tells a select story of his life, his experiences going to Princeton, being hired by a small, select financial company in Manhattan, and meeting and falling for a young American woman named Erica.<br /><br />The story of Changez and Erica is very strange, doubly strange when folded into this sort of narrative style. I think I'll remember it for quite a while.<br /><br /><b>but all those contentious reviews ...</b><br /><br />I'm sure the low ratings of many have nothing to do with the literary merits of the novel. They have to do with the attitudes toward America that Changez slowly reveals throughout his telling, attitudes which in fact he only becomes aware of as certain incidents occur which evoke (as he tells it) surprise on his own part, when he realizes how he has reacted. I don't believe I'll go into any specifics about this, but I found his recounting of these attitudes very believable from the point of view of a person from that part of the world.<br /><br />The story is something of a mystery – a mystery with at least two, perhaps more, ominous threads which slowly are revealed and slowly grow darker.<br /><br />You may dislike the narrator (I didn’t), but that doesn’t seem to be the point anyway, to me. And, even if the author himself is taken as having the same attitudes as the narrator does, well, to dislike the story because one dislikes the author doesn’t seem to be a way of judging literature either.<br /><br />And it is literature, not a political essay. In many ways, for many reasons, an unforgettable novel.<br /><br /><br />- - - - - - - - - - - - - - -<br /><br /><b>Previous review:</b> <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1965388287" rel="nofollow noopener">The Taming of the Shrew</a><br /><b>Random review:</b> <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/267406536" rel="nofollow noopener">The Whistling Season</a> <i>Ivan Doig</i><br /><b>Next review:</b> <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1233303892" rel="nofollow noopener">Organic Marxism: An Alternative to Capitalism and Ecological Catastrophe</a><br /><br /><b>Previous library review:</b> <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/270144655" rel="nofollow noopener">Invisible Man</a> <i>Ralph Ellison</i><br /><b>Next library review:</b> <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/249148814" rel="nofollow noopener">Dune</a></span>

<div id="review_335009887" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Garima" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7915003-garima"><img alt="Garima" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1360053697p2/7915003.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7915003-garima?shelf=my-2-cents">my-2-cents</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer335009887" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2964938409267924188"><b>An Open Letter to America</b><br/><br/>which unfortunately I read late, around 5 years late. Why unfortunate? B’coz I might have liked it or probably loved it since I was a naive reader back then i.e I was into Sheldons and Archers and closer home Bhagats *blushes*. Anyway, I was well aware when this book hit the literary world and took it by storm. A dashing title, a Pakistani author, a reluctant subject, a movie in the making by Mira Nair and that’s precisely the reason I wanted to read the book before watc</span>
  <span id="freeText2964938409267924188" style="display:none"><b>An Open Letter to America</b><br /><br />which unfortunately I read late, around 5 years late. Why unfortunate? B’coz I might have liked it or probably loved it since I was a naive reader back then i.e I was into Sheldons and Archers and closer home Bhagats *blushes*. Anyway, I was well aware when this book hit the literary world and took it by storm. A dashing title, a Pakistani author, a reluctant subject, a movie in the making by Mira Nair and that’s precisely the reason I wanted to read the book before watching the movie , so bought a copy and was yayyyy...finally! Great read it’s gonna be!!<br /><br />1st page: ok.<br /><br />2nd page: yeah okk.<br /><br />3rd page: Ahan! I know where you are heading.<br /><br />50th page: err..no, I don’t know where you are heading, but I sense a twist just around the corner.<br /><br />100th page: A love story..,girl’s lover dead...can’t forget him...a clinic...I once had a girl...Norwegian wood…Yes! Yes!...No! OK.<br /><br />183rd page: just few more lines and then contact Agent J a.k.a. Will Smith and request for the memory eraser toy and move on to your next Murakami read. <br /><br />And Nooo!! (Kindly excuse the superlatives) I didn’t hate this book but hated the fact as to why I wasn’t able to appreciate it in any way possible. It made me uncomfortable throughout rather than excited and the most irritating part is that you are compelled to read it till the end in the hope of getting hold of the whole idea behind this book. At the end, the author hurled a very smart curve ball towards his readers, leaving most of us in dilemmas, some on the side of Changez (the protagonist), some on the side of Mr. America (envying that delectable Lahori food he had) and some wishing to watch the re-run of 2011 epic cricket world cup semi-final between India and Pakistan and marveling at its brilliance and that moment when..Aargh! I never knew writing the review would be a similar experience like that of reading this book..distracting!! <br /><br />This is the second book I read by a Pakistani author, first being <i>My Feudal Lord</i> by Tehmina Durrani, which I judged on the basis of its subject and not on writing style and since I read it around 6 years ago, all I could recall was that it was simple but affected me enough to evoke emotions of empathy which might not hold true at present having read many great books and becoming more aware and objective about the world around me since then so it might not feature in the league of extra ordinary but it definitely left an impression which reluctant fundamentalist, as I highly doubt would be able to achieve. As the story was unfolding it became, hardly audible and incredibly distant. And the writing style!! I wish the narration was in one to one style as it started bothering me after few chapters, may be the execution was unconvincing or plain dull *oxymoron*. <br /><br />This book has some great ideas but somehow fell short of the elements that would have made it a great page turner. It felt too safe and too confined for my taste. Islamic Fundamentalism is a sensitive subject and needs to be handled carefully without actually conveying any negative message or an ambiguous one but what Mohsin Hamid as seemed, resisted from going out of his comfort zone and stating everything at a superficial level without actually diving deep.<br /> <br />The only thing I found acceptable was his realization of being victimized or prone to victimization because <i>“I am a Muslim”,</i> but like I stated that I read it a bit late so in today’s time this has become a bit redundant and again not helping in scoring brownie points for Mr. Hamid. <br /><br />Those stars are simply because as a writer he definitely has potential provided he let himself go of all the inhibitions if he’s having any, from his literary genes.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_873326027" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/873326027" />
    <a title="Jibran" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5407952-jibran"><img alt="Jibran" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572721231p2/5407952.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/873326027?book_show_action=true">Mar 06, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jibran" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jibran" href="/user/show/5407952-jibran">Jibran</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780151013043" href="/book/show/88815.The_Reluctant_Fundamentalist">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5407952-jibran?shelf=south-asian-fiction">south-asian-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer873326027" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6724921484388610183"><i>“When my turn came, I said I hoped one day to be the dictator of an Islamic republic with nuclear capability; the others appeared shocked, and I was forced to explain that I had been joking.” </i><br/><br/>It explores, but only manages to scratch the surface, the question of religious fundamentalism amongst contemporary Muslim youth. No, it doesn't explore it, but makes a joke out of it, through an artificially constructed dilemma of one Changez, a Pakistani expat in the United States, who has turned to "fund</span>
  <span id="freeText6724921484388610183" style="display:none"><i>“When my turn came, I said I hoped one day to be the dictator of an Islamic republic with nuclear capability; the others appeared shocked, and I was forced to explain that I had been joking.” </i><br /><br />It explores, but only manages to scratch the surface, the question of religious fundamentalism amongst contemporary Muslim youth. No, it doesn't explore it, but makes a joke out of it, through an artificially constructed dilemma of one Changez, a Pakistani expat in the United States, who has turned to "fundamentalism" after the history-making day of nine-eleven.<br /><br />Location: Lahore, the famous Food Street in Old city. The Reluctant Fundamentalist dines with an anonymous person about whom the only information we get is his nationality: he's an American man. How and why he's here we don't get to find out. The American seems like a phantasmal installation - a dummy of sorts - to lend our Reluctant Fundamentalist an ear.<br /><br />The narrative is almost entirely made up of a monologue; the reader is not allowed to hear the reactions of the American stranger. Changez speaks continuously as he recounts his experiences of student life in the US. Through this unimpressive frame story, as though a flippant Conrad gone berserk, we enter the main story. <br /><br />Changez has a common migrant story. He goes to study at Princeton (yes it is always Princeton or Harvard or Oxford or Cambridge - it seems fictional characters don't go to medium-tier universities but that's a non-sequitur!), lands in a lucrative finance job (again, it's either finance and engineering or taxis and toilets, but that's a non-sequitur too!); he's doing well in every way but loses his intellectual and emotional moorings when the planes strike the Twin Towers on that fateful, ridiculous morning.<br /><br />The ensuing American invasions unhinge Changez. He begins to doubt himself (he just starts doubting, without going through a process of introspection which, included, would have lent some credibility to the narrative), his loyalties change, his outlook on life undergoes a drastic no-angled turn, and he finds himself questioning his life in the United States. This break is symbolically represented by Changez's relationship with an American girl 'Erica', who is actually 'America' - once his beloved, now an undesired castaway. They have one good sex, a mutual orgasm, and then they go separate ways.<br /><br />It is not so much a tale of a truly reluctant fundamentalist than a person torn between what he sees as two mutually exclusive sets of loyalties. Changez suffers from an identity crisis and religious fundamentalism only makes up a silly excuse. There's nothing in his new outlook that confirms his born-again religiosity. He drinks, gambles, fornicates with his girlfriend, and doesn’t seem to have a moral issue with his lifestyle as one might expect of an Islamic fundamentalist who rejects liberal lifestyles. His opposition to American warmongering is political not religious. He talks about “our Asia” instead of “our Ummah” or the global Muslim community. This gives us room for interpretation but we do get the message don't we...<br /><br />All in all, it's a fast read, enjoyable for its humour, but nothing much apart from that, and it doesn't require of you to think much before you have finished reading the slender novella. Mohsin Hamid wrote it before 9/11 but when it happened, edited it to make it relevant, sold millions of copies and hurray, made a fortune! But if a work of fiction depends so much on day-to-day history, it simply means that it's destined to last as long as the hype lasts. <br /><br />April 2015</span>
  <a data-text-id="6724921484388610183" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="W" class="user" itemprop="url" name="W" href="/user/show/49365462-w">W</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780151013043" href="/book/show/88815.The_Reluctant_Fundamentalist">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/49365462-w?shelf=pakistani-fiction">pakistani-fiction</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1451817500" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3252275835203184489">Generally,I'm a bit wary of Booker nominees,but in this case they got it right.It would have been a lot better if this book had actually won the Booker Prize,instead of merely being shortlisted.<br/><br/>However,the nomination generated quite a buzz,and introduced me to Mohsin Hamid.And oh boy,his first two books were very impressive.<br/><br/>I read it in one sitting,a short and very interesting book,which held my interest from the very first page to the last.It explores a young Pakistani man's drift into extremis</span>
  <span id="freeText3252275835203184489" style="display:none">Generally,I'm a bit wary of Booker nominees,but in this case they got it right.It would have been a lot better if this book had actually won the Booker Prize,instead of merely being shortlisted.<br /><br />However,the nomination generated quite a buzz,and introduced me to Mohsin Hamid.And oh boy,his first two books were very impressive.<br /><br />I read it in one sitting,a short and very interesting book,which held my interest from the very first page to the last.It explores a young Pakistani man's drift into extremism,after he has spent a good part of his life studying and working in the US.<br /><br />There is one thing though,the protagonist Changez,doesn't really appear the type to become a fundamentalist,so suddenly,as 9/11 happens.He has a good job and is well settled in the US.<br /><br />However,the book reminded me of the real life case of Faisal Shehzad,a Pakistani man who was arrested in the US in 2010 for trying to blow something up.But Faisal Shehzad was struggling with his job and had already become radicalized.<br /><br />With this book,Mohsin Hamid took a gamble.It could easily have flopped,given the sensitivity of the subject and the anti Muslim feeling in the West,following 9/11 and the actions of the hijackers.Instead,it became a bestseller.<br /><br />It is a fascinating book,one which also depicts the dilemmas of those caught between two worlds,the East and the West.They don't quite fit in either world.<br /><br />It is just too bad that after this book,the quality of Mohsin Hamid's writing went downhill,as far as I am concerned.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3252275835203184489" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1191245937" />
    <a title="Jason" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5278185-jason"><img alt="Jason" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1553537834p2/5278185.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1191245937?book_show_action=true">Feb 04, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason" href="/user/show/5278185-jason">Jason</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780385678827" href="/book/show/17284571-the-reluctant-fundamentalist">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1191245937" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16803984575067004313">Wow. This is everything I want in a novel. Engaging and somewhat experimental narration and challenging politics. I plan to make a discussion video about this book, so I'll save a lot of my thoughts, but let me say that this was brilliant.<br/><br/>The second-person narration is extremely powerful, as it confronts "you"—the implied American (or Western) reader—and implicates you directly in the events that have taken place in the novel and as complicit with the politics that shaped the landscape that prod</span>
  <span id="freeText16803984575067004313" style="display:none">Wow. This is everything I want in a novel. Engaging and somewhat experimental narration and challenging politics. I plan to make a discussion video about this book, so I'll save a lot of my thoughts, but let me say that this was brilliant.<br /><br />The second-person narration is extremely powerful, as it confronts "you"—the implied American (or Western) reader—and implicates you directly in the events that have taken place in the novel and as complicit with the politics that shaped the landscape that produced it. It is a post-9/11 novel, fascinating because the second-person narrative, a conversation between a Pakistani man, Changez, and an unnamed American, is entirely one-sided; the American character speaks, but we don't have access to his words. Everything is mediated through Changez, the entire story is told through his perspective, and the American voice is ostensibly erased, especially interesting considering the discourse in post-9/11 society that has attempted to erase any dissenting voice.<br /><br />Finally, the ending, so ambiguous and heavy with discomfort, is so challenging and so productive. This was a brilliant reading experience for me. I can't wait to talk more about it.<br /><br />"As a society, you were unwilling to reflect upon the shared pain that united you with those who attacked you. You retreated into myths of your own difference, assumptions of your own superiority. And you acted out these beliefs on the stage of the world, so that the entire planet was rocked by the repercussions of your tantrums, not least my family, now facing war thousands of miles away. Such an America had to be stopped in the interests not only of the rest of humanity, but also in your own."</span>
  <a data-text-id="16803984575067004313" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2283853622?book_show_action=true">Jan 09, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dannii Elle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dannii Elle" href="/user/show/44487975-dannii-elle">Dannii Elle</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141029542" href="/book/show/2163164.The_Reluctant_Fundamentalist">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44487975-dannii-elle?shelf=contemporary-cuteness">contemporary-cuteness</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2283853622" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15592351325004882414">Whilst delivering one man's story, Mohsin Hamid introduces the reader to an entire nation's. This clever allegory defies traditional structure, with its unique narrative style, and transcends emotion, by seeming to produce a severe lack of it. And this frank display of truth invites the reader to query their own.<br/><br/>This is a story everyone feels they have read, in one format or another, but Hamid tears down the boundaries of this known narrative to deliver a truth everyone needs to read.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2564779918" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2564779918" />
    <a title="Ankit Garg" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5181592-ankit-garg"><img alt="Ankit Garg" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1446406366p2/5181592.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2564779918?book_show_action=true">May 16, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ankit Garg" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ankit Garg" href="/user/show/5181592-ankit-garg">Ankit Garg</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2564779918" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14049360422004509219">The Reluctant Fundamentalist by Mohsin Hamid is a short-yet-thought-provoking read about the after-effects of 9/11. It is a first person narrative of a Pakistani Muslim residing in the States, and how his life gets tougher every passing day after the attack.<br/><br/>With a subtle and unique narration style, the book does not fail to impress. The change in the attitude of the protagonist from a moderate Muslim to a hard-core one in the wake of the terrorist attack due to transformations in his personal an</span>
  <span id="freeText14049360422004509219" style="display:none">The Reluctant Fundamentalist by Mohsin Hamid is a short-yet-thought-provoking read about the after-effects of 9/11. It is a first person narrative of a Pakistani Muslim residing in the States, and how his life gets tougher every passing day after the attack.<br /><br />With a subtle and unique narration style, the book does not fail to impress. The change in the attitude of the protagonist from a moderate Muslim to a hard-core one in the wake of the terrorist attack due to transformations in his personal and professional life is subtly depicted in the story.<br /><br />If you are aware about the awfully difficult language used in the books nominated for Booker, you'll be pleased to know that it is not the case with this book, and which is a welcome move more on the Booker's part than on that of the author's, if you know what I mean. <br /><br />Verdict: Read it for the narration style.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14049360422004509219" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review2564779918">
